<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Coal</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Combustible sedimentary rock composed primarily of carbon</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Coal_(disambiguation)" title="Coal (disambiguation)">Coal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Coal</caption><tbody><tr><td <ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Coal</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combustible" title="Combustible">combustible</a> black or brownish-black <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a>, formed as <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">rock strata</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_seams" title="Coal seams">coal seams</a>. Coal is mostly <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> with variable amounts of other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a>, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
Coal is formed when dead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_matter" title="Plant matter">plant matter</a> decays into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> and is converted into coal by the heat and pressure of deep burial over millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_Coal_Explained_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIA_Coal_Explained-2">[2]</a></sup> Vast deposits of coal originate in former <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>—called <a href="/wiki/Coal_forest" title="Coal forest">coal forests</a>—that covered much of the Earth's tropical land areas during the late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvanian_(geology)" title="Pennsylvanian (geology)">Pennsylvanian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClealThomas2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClealThomas2005-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-4">[4]</a></sup> However, many significant coal deposits are younger than this and originate from the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> eras.
</p><p>Coal is primarily used as a fuel. While coal has been known and used for thousands of years, its usage was limited until the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. With the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>, coal consumption increased. In 2020 coal supplied about a quarter of the world's <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> and over a third of its <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEAstats2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEAstats2-5">[5]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> making and other industrial processes burn coal.
</p><p>The extraction and use of coal causes premature deaths and illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heal_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heal-6">[6]</a></sup> The use of coal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">damages the environment</a>, and it is the largest <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anthropogenic" title="wikt:anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a> source of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> contributing to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. 14 billion tonnes of carbon dioxide was emitted by burning coal in 2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cebf_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-cebf-7">[7]</a></sup> which is 40% of the total fossil fuel emissions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys2018_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-phys2018-8">[8]</a></sup> and over 25% of total global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> As part of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Energy_transition" title="Energy transition">energy transition</a> many countries have <a href="/wiki/Coal_phase-out" title="Coal phase-out">reduced or eliminated their use of coal power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Secretary_General" title="UN Secretary General">UN Secretary General</a> asked governments to stop building new <a href="/wiki/Coal-fired_power_station" title="Coal-fired power station">coal plants</a> by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Global <a href="/wiki/Peak_coal" title="Peak coal">coal use peaked</a> in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup> To meet the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> target of keeping <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> to below 2 °C (3.6 °F) <a href="/wiki/Coal_phase-out" title="Coal phase-out">coal use needs to halve</a> from 2020 to 2030,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and phasing down coal was agreed in the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Climate_Pact" title="Glasgow Climate Pact">Glasgow Climate Pact</a>.
</p><p>The largest consumer and importer of coal in 2020 was <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_China" title="Coal in China">China</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_China" title="Coal mining in China">China accounts for almost half the world's annual coal production</a>, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> with about a tenth. <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Indonesia#Coal" title="Energy in Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> export the most, followed by <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Russia" title="Coal in Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word originally took the form <i>col</i> in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>kula</i>(<i>n</i>), which in turn is hypothesized to come from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> root *<i>g</i>(<i>e</i>)<i>u-lo-</i> "live coal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> cognates include the <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>kole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>cole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>kool</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>chol</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Kohle</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>kol</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> word <i>gual</i> is also a cognate via the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-etym-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h2>
<p>Coal is composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macerals" title="Macerals">macerals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">minerals</a> and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGS_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-BGS-17">[17]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">Fossils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> may be found in coal.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/220px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/330px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/440px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example chemical structure of coal</div></div></div>
<p>The conversion of dead vegetation into coal is called <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/coalification" title="wikt:coalification">coalification</a>. At various times in the geologic past, the Earth had dense forests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> in low-lying wetland areas. In these wetlands, the process of coalification began when dead plant matter was protected from <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>, usually by mud or acidic water, and was converted into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>. This trapped the carbon in immense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peat_bog" title="Peat bog">peat bogs</a> that were eventually deeply buried by sediments. Then, over millions of years, the heat and pressure of deep burial caused the loss of water, methane and carbon dioxide and increased in the proportion of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGS_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-BGS-17">[17]</a></sup> The grade of coal produced depended on the maximum pressure and temperature reached, with <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> (also called "brown coal") produced under relatively mild conditions, and <a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">sub-bituminous coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite coal</a> (also called "hard coal" or "black coal") produced in turn with increasing temperature and pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_Coal_Explained_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-EIA_Coal_Explained-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Of the factors involved in coalification, temperature is much more important than either pressure or time of burial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Subbituminous coal can form at temperatures as low as 35 to 80 °C (95 to 176 °F) while anthracite requires a temperature of at least 180 to 245 °C (356 to 473 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although coal is known from most geologic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_(geology)" title="Period (geology)">periods</a>, 90% of all coal beds were deposited in the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> periods, which represent just 2% of the Earth's geologic history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Paradoxically, this was during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Paleozoic_icehouse" title="Late Paleozoic icehouse">Late Paleozoic icehouse</a>, a time of global <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">glaciation</a>. However, the drop in global sea level accompanying the glaciation exposed <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelfs</a> that had previously been submerged, and to these were added wide <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">river deltas</a> produced by increased <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> due to the drop in <a href="/wiki/Base_level" title="Base level">base level</a>. These widespread areas of wetlands provided ideal conditions for coal formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201888–92_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201888–92-23">[23]</a></sup> The rapid formation of coal ended with the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event#Coal_Gap" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">coal gap</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, where coal is rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retallack1996_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Retallack1996-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Favorable geography alone does not explain the extensive Carboniferous coal beds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201899_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201899-25">[25]</a></sup> Other factors contributing to rapid coal deposition were high <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> levels, above 30%, that promoted intense <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> and formation of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> that was all but indigestible by decomposing organisms; high <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> levels that promoted plant growth; and the nature of Carboniferous forests, which included <a href="/wiki/Lycophyte" title="Lycophyte">lycophyte</a> trees whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Determinate_growth" title="Determinate growth">determinate growth</a> meant that carbon was not tied up in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heartwood" title="Heartwood">heartwood</a> of living trees for long periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201898–102_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGhee201898–102-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>One theory suggested that about 360 million years ago, some plants evolved the ability to produce <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, a complex polymer that made their <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> stems much harder and more woody. The ability to produce lignin led to the evolution of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trees" title="Trees">trees</a>. But bacteria and fungi did not immediately evolve the ability to decompose lignin, so the wood did not fully decay but became buried under sediment, eventually turning into coal. About 300 million years ago, mushrooms and other fungi developed this ability, ending the main coal-formation period of earth's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Although some authors pointed at some evidence of lignin degradation during the Carboniferous, and suggested that climatic and tectonic factors were a more plausible explanation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> reconstruction of ancestral enzymes by phylogenetic analysis corrobarated a hypothesis that lignin degrading enzymes appeared in fungi approximately 200 MYa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>One likely tectonic factor was the <a href="/wiki/Central_Pangean_Mountains" title="Central Pangean Mountains">Central Pangean Mountains</a>, an enormous range running along the equator that reached its greatest elevation near this time. Climate modeling suggests that the Central Pangean Mountains contributed to the deposition of vast quantities of coal in the late Carboniferous. The mountains created an area of year-round heavy precipitation, with no dry season typical of a <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> climate. This is necessary for the preservation of peat in coal swamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal is known from <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> strata, which predate land plants. This coal is presumed to have originated from residues of algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler1957_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler1957-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancuso1981_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mancuso1981-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes coal seams (also known as coal beds) are interbedded with other sediments in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclothem" title="Cyclothem">cyclothem</a>. Cyclothems are thought have their origin in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_cycle" title="Glacial cycle">glacial cycles</a> that produced fluctuations in <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>, which alternately exposed and then flooded large areas of continental shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_of_coalification">Chemistry of coalification</span></h4>
<p>The woody tissue of plants is composed mainly of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin. Modern peat is mostly lignin, with a content of cellulose and hemicellulose ranging from 5% to 40%. Various other organic compounds, such as waxes and nitrogen- and sulfur-containing compounds, are also present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Lignin has a weight composition of about 54% carbon, 6% hydrogen, and 30% oxygen, while cellulose has a weight composition of about 44% carbon, 6% hydrogen, and 49% oxygen. Bituminous coal has a composition of about 84.4% carbon, 5.4% hydrogen, 6.7% oxygen, 1.7% nitrogen, and 1.8% sulfur, on a weight basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perry-36">[36]</a></sup> This implies that chemical processes during coalification must remove most of the oxygen and much of the hydrogen, leaving carbon, a process called <i>carbonization</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbonization proceeds primarily by <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a>, and demethanation. Dehydration removes water molecules from the maturing coal via reactions such as<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatcher-etal-1992_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-hatcher-etal-1992-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 R–OH → R–O–R + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>2 R-CH2-O-CH2-R → R-CH=CH-R + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Decarboxylation removes carbon dioxide from the maturing coal and proceeds by reaction such as<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatcher-etal-1992_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-hatcher-etal-1992-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>RCOOH  →  RH  +  CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>while demethanation proceeds by reaction such as
</p>
<dl><dd>2 R-CH<sub>3</sub> → R-CH<sub>2</sub>-R + CH<sub>4</sub></dd>
<dd>R-CH<sub>2</sub>-CH<sub>2</sub>-CH<sub>2</sub>-R → R-CH=CH-R + CH<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>In each of these formulas, R represents the remainder of a cellulose or lignin molecule to which the reacting groups are attached.
</p><p>Dehydration and decarboxylation take place early in coalification, while demethanation begins only after the coal has already reached bituminous rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The effect of decarboxylation is to reduce the percentage of oxygen, while demethanation reduces the percentage of hydrogen. Dehydration does both, and (together with demethanation) reduces the saturation of the carbon backbone (increasing the number of double bonds between carbon).
</p><p>As carbonization proceeds, <a href="/wiki/Aliphatic_compound" title="Aliphatic compound">aliphatic compounds</a> (carbon compounds characterized by chains of carbon atoms) are replaced by <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_compound" title="Aromatic compound">aromatic compounds</a> (carbon compounds characterized by rings of carbon atoms) and aromatic rings begin to fuse into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyaromatic" title="Polyaromatic">polyaromatic</a> compounds (linked rings of carbon atoms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The structure increasingly resembles <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a>, the structural element of graphite.
</p><p>Chemical changes are accompanied by physical changes, such as decrease in average pore size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The macerals (organic particles) of lignite are composed of <i>huminite</i>, which is earthy in appearance. As the coal matures to sub-bituminous coal, huminite begins to be replaced by vitreous (shiny) <i>vitrinite</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Maturation of bituminous coal is characterized by <i>bitumenization</i>, in which part of the coal is converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>, a hydrocarbon-rich gel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Maturation to anthracite is characterized by <i>debitumenization</i> (from demethanation) and the increasing tendency of the anthracite to break with a <a href="/wiki/Conchoidal_fracture" title="Conchoidal fracture">conchoidal fracture</a>, similar to the way thick glass breaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Ranks"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/220px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/330px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/440px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal exposure of the Point Aconi Seam in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2665" data-file-width="2132" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/300px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/450px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/600px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal ranking system used by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a></div></div></div>
<p>As geological processes apply <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> to dead <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> over time, under suitable conditions, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metamorphic_grade" title="Metamorphic grade">metamorphic grade</a> or rank increases successively into:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a>, a precursor of coal</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">Lignite</a>, or brown coal, the lowest rank of coal, most harmful to health,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> used almost exclusively as fuel for electric power generation
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jet_(lignite)" title="Jet (lignite)">Jet</a>, a compact form of lignite, sometimes polished; used as an ornamental stone since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Palaeolithic" title="Upper Palaeolithic">Upper Palaeolithic</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">Sub-bituminous coal</a>, whose properties range between those of lignite and those of bituminous coal, is used primarily as fuel for steam-electric power generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a>, a dense sedimentary rock, usually black, but sometimes dark brown, often with well-defined bands of bright and dull material. It is used primarily as fuel in steam-electric power generation and to make <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>. Known as steam coal in the UK, and historically used to raise steam in steam locomotives and ships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">Anthracite coal</a>, the highest rank of coal, is a harder, glossy black coal used primarily for residential and commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> is difficult to ignite and not commonly used as fuel; it is most used in pencils, or powdered for <a href="/wiki/Lubrication" title="Lubrication">lubrication</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">Cannel coal</a> (sometimes called "candle coal") is a variety of fine-grained, high-rank coal with significant hydrogen content, which consists primarily of <a href="/wiki/Liptinite" title="Liptinite">liptinite</a>.</li></ul>
<p>There are several international standards for coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The classification of coal is generally based on the content of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_volatiles" title="Coal volatiles">volatiles</a>. However the most important distinction is between thermal coal (also known as steam coal), which is burnt to generate electricity via steam; and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_coal" title="Metallurgical coal">metallurgical coal</a> (also known as coking coal), which is burnt at high temperature to make <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt's law">Hilt's law</a> is a geological observation that (within a small area) the deeper the coal is found, the higher its rank (or grade). It applies if the thermal gradient is entirely vertical; however, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphism</a> may cause lateral changes of rank, irrespective of depth. For example, some of the coal seams of the <a href="/wiki/Madrid,_New_Mexico" title="Madrid, New Mexico">Madrid, New Mexico</a> coal field were partially converted to anthracite by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contact_metamorphism" title="Contact metamorphism">contact metamorphism</a> from an igneous <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">sill</a> while the remainder of the seams remained as bituminous coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">History of coal mining</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="212" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif/170px-Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese coal miners in an illustration of the <i>Tiangong Kaiwu</i> encyclopedia, published in 1637</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest recognized use is from the <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Shenyang</a> area of China where by 4000 BC <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> inhabitants had begun carving ornaments from black lignite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Coal from the <a href="/wiki/Fushun" title="Fushun">Fushun</a> mine in northeastern China was used to smelt <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> as early as 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, the Italian who traveled to China in the 13th century, described coal as "black stones ... which burn like logs", and said coal was so plentiful, people could take three hot baths a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In Europe, the earliest reference to the use of coal as fuel is from the geological treatise <i>On Stones</i> (Lap. 16) by the Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (c. 371–287 BC):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mattusch_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mattusch-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Among the materials that are dug because they are useful, those known as <i>anthrakes</i> [coals] are made of earth, and, once set on fire, they burn like charcoal [anthrakes]. They are found in Liguria ... and in Elis as one approaches Olympia by the mountain road; and they are used by those who work in metals.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Theophrastus, On Stones (16) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p><span class="anchor" id="outcrop_coal"></span><a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">Outcrop</a> coal was used in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (3000–2000 BC), where it formed part of <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Pyre" title="Pyre">pyres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, with the exception of two modern fields, "the <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Roman_Britain" title="Mining in Roman Britain">Romans</a> were exploiting coals in all the major coalfields in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> by the end of the second century AD".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-56">[56]</a></sup> Evidence of trade in coal, dated to about AD 200, has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Heronbridge_Roman_Site" title="Heronbridge Roman Site">Roman settlement at Heronbridge</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a>; and in the <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, where coal from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Midlands" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a> was transported via the <a href="/wiki/Car_Dyke" title="Car Dyke">Car Dyke</a> for use in drying grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Coal cinders have been found in the hearths of <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_forts" title="Roman forts">Roman forts</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, dated to around AD 400. In the west of England, contemporary writers described the wonder of a permanent brazier of coal on the altar of <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aquae_Sulis" title="Aquae Sulis">Aquae Sulis</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>), although in fact easily accessible surface coal from what became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somerset_coalfield" title="Somerset coalfield">Somerset coalfield</a> was in common use in quite lowly dwellings locally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Evidence of coal's use for iron-working in the city during the Roman period has been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eschweiler" title="Eschweiler">Eschweiler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a> were used by the Romans for the smelting of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/220px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/330px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/440px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal miner in Britain, 1942</div></div></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Sea_coal"></span>
No evidence exists of coal being of great importance in Britain before about AD 1000, the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-60">[60]</a></sup> Coal came to be referred to as "seacoal" in the 13th century; the wharf where the material arrived in London was known as Seacoal Lane, so identified in a charter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">King Henry III</a> granted in 1253.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Initially, the name was given because much coal was found on the shore, having fallen from the exposed coal seams on cliffs above or washed out of underwater coal outcrops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-60">[60]</a></sup> but by the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, it was understood to derive from the way it was carried to London by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1257–1259, coal from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a> was shipped to London for the <a href="/wiki/Metalsmith" title="Metalsmith">smiths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>-burners building <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-60">[60]</a></sup> Seacoal Lane and Newcastle Lane, where coal was unloaded at wharves along the <a href="/wiki/River_Fleet" title="River Fleet">River Fleet</a>, still exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>These easily accessible sources had largely become exhausted (or could not meet the growing demand) by the 13th century, when underground extraction by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">shaft mining</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adit" title="Adit">adits</a> was developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-54">[54]</a></sup> The alternative name was "pitcoal", because it came from mines.
</p><p>Cooking and home heating with coal (in addition to firewood or instead of it) has been done in various times and places throughout human history, especially in times and places where ground-surface coal was available and firewood was scarce, but a widespread reliance on coal for home hearths probably never existed until such a switch in fuels happened in London in the late sixteenth and early seventeenth centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman-2020_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-2020-64">[64]</a></sup> Historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruth_Goodman_(historian)" title="Ruth Goodman (historian)">Ruth Goodman</a> has traced the socioeconomic effects of that switch and its later spread throughout Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman-2020_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-2020-64">[64]</a></sup> and suggested that its importance in shaping the industrial adoption of coal has been previously underappreciated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman-2020_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-2020-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: xiv–xix">: xiv–xix </span></sup>
</p><p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> led to the large-scale use of coal, as the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> took over from the <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a>. In 1700, five-sixths of the world's coal was mined in Britain. Britain would have run out of suitable sites for watermills by the 1830s if coal had not been available as a source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wrigley_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigley-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1947 there were some 750,000 miners in Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-66">[66]</a></sup> but the last deep coal mine in the UK closed in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>A grade between bituminous coal and anthracite was once known as "steam coal" as it was widely used as a fuel for <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotives</a>. In this specialized use, it is sometimes known as "sea coal" in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Small "steam coal", also called <i>dry small steam nuts</i> (or DSSN), was used as a fuel for domestic <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>.
</p><p>Coal played an important role in industry in the 19th and 20th century. The predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Coal_and_Steel_Community" title="European Coal and Steel Community">European Coal and Steel Community</a>, was based on the trading of this commodity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal continues to arrive on beaches around the world from both natural erosion of exposed coal seams and windswept spills from cargo ships. Many homes in such areas gather this coal as a significant, and sometimes primary, source of home heating fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h3>
<p>The composition of coal is reported either as a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Proximate_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Proximate analysis (page does not exist)">proximate analysis</a> (moisture, volatile matter, fixed carbon, and ash) or an <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ultimate_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ultimate analysis (page does not exist)">ultimate analysis</a> (ash, carbon, hydrogen, nitrogen, oxygen, and sulfur). The "volatile matter" does not exist by itself (except for some adsorbed methane) but designates the volatile compounds that are produced and driven off by heating the coal. A typical bituminous coal may have an ultimate analysis on a dry, ash-free basis of 84.4% carbon, 5.4% hydrogen, 6.7% oxygen, 1.7% nitrogen, and 1.8% sulfur, on a weight basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perry-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The composition of ash, given in terms of oxides, varies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Perry-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coking_coal_and_use_of_coke_to_smelt_iron">Coking coal and use of coke to smelt iron</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke (fuel)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="3039" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/220px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/330px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/440px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coke oven at a <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_fuel" title="Smokeless fuel">smokeless fuel</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, United Kingdom</div></div></div>
<p>Coke is a solid carbonaceous residue derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coking_coal" title="Coking coal">coking coal</a> (a low-ash, low-sulfur bituminous coal, also known as <i>metallurgical coal</i>), which is used in manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> and other iron products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-75">[75]</a></sup> Coke is made from coking coal by baking in an oven without oxygen at temperatures as high as 1,000 °C, driving off the volatile constituents and fusing together the fixed carbon and residual ash. Metallurgical coke is used as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> and as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>  The carbon monoxide produced by its combustion reduces <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.
</p><p>Waste carbon dioxide is also produced <span class="nowrap">(<span class="mwe-math-element" id="2Fe2O3 + 3C"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {2Fe2O3 + 3C -&gt; 4Fe + 3CO2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" id="2Fe2O3_+_3C" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f48752473973ce8b21bd34db10ddeedea6072d35" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:32.21ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>)</span> together with <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, which is too rich in dissolved carbon so must be treated further to make steel.
</p><p>Coking coal should be low in ash, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, so that these do not migrate to the metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-75">[75]</a></sup>
The coke must be <a href="/wiki/Coke_strength_after_reaction" title="Coke strength after reaction">strong enough</a> to resist the weight of overburden in the blast furnace, which is why coking coal is so important in making steel using the conventional route. Coke from coal is grey, hard, and porous and has a heating value of 29.6 MJ/kg. Some cokemaking processes produce byproducts, including <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, light oils, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a> (petcoke) is the solid residue obtained in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_refining" title="Oil refining">oil refining</a>, which resembles coke but contains too many impurities to be useful in metallurgical applications.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_foundry_components">Use in foundry components <span class="anchor" id="Industrial_processes"></span></span></h4>
<p>Finely ground bituminous coal, known in this application as sea coal, is a constituent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundry_sand" title="Foundry sand">foundry sand</a>. While the molten metal is in the <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">mould</a>, the coal burns slowly, releasing <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing gases</a> at pressure, and so preventing the metal from penetrating the pores of the sand. It is also contained in 'mould wash', a paste or liquid with the same function applied to the mould before casting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Sea coal can be mixed with the clay lining (the "bod") used for the bottom of a <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">cupola furnace</a>. When heated, the coal decomposes and the bod becomes slightly friable, easing the process of breaking open holes for tapping the molten metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_coke">Alternatives to coke</span></h4>
<p>Scrap steel can be recycled in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>; and an alternative to making iron by smelting is <a href="/wiki/Direct_reduced_iron" title="Direct reduced iron">direct reduced iron</a>, where any carbonaceous fuel can be used to make sponge or pelletised iron. To lessen carbon dioxide emissions <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> can be used as the reducing agent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> or waste as the source of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Historically, charcoal has been used as an alternative to coke in a blast furnace, with the resultant iron being known as <a href="/wiki/Charcoal_iron" title="Charcoal iron">charcoal iron</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gasification">Gasification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">Coal gasification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underground_coal_gasification" title="Underground coal gasification">Underground coal gasification</a></div>
<p>Coal gasification, as part of an <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) coal-fired power station, is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO) and hydrogen (H<sub>2</sub>) gas to fire gas turbines to produce electricity. Syngas can also be converted into transportation fuels, such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch_process" title="Fischer–Tropsch process">Fischer–Tropsch process</a>; alternatively, syngas can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, which can be blended into fuel directly or converted to gasoline via the methanol to gasoline process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Gasification combined with Fischer–Tropsch technology was used by the <a href="/wiki/Sasol" title="Sasol">Sasol</a> chemical company of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to make chemicals and motor vehicle fuels from coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>During gasification, the coal is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> while also being heated and pressurized. During the reaction, oxygen and water molecules <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidize</a> the coal into carbon monoxide (CO), while also releasing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas (H<sub>2</sub>). This used to be done in underground coal mines, and also to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_gas" title="Town gas">town gas</a>, which was piped to customers to burn for illumination, heating, and cooking.
</p>
<dl><dd>3C (<i>as Coal</i>) + O<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub> + 3CO</dd></dl>
<p>If the refiner wants to produce gasoline, the syngas is routed into a Fischer–Tropsch reaction. This is known as indirect coal liquefaction. If hydrogen is the desired end-product, however, the syngas is fed into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, where more hydrogen is liberated:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquefaction">Liquefaction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a></div>
<p>Coal can be converted directly into <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> equivalent to gasoline or diesel by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Coal liquefaction emits more carbon dioxide than liquid fuel production from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>. Mixing in biomass and using CCS would emit slightly less than the oil process but at a high cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> State owned <a href="/wiki/China_Energy_Investment" title="China Energy Investment">China Energy Investment</a> runs a coal liquefaction plant and plans to build 2 more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal liquefaction may also refer to the cargo hazard when shipping coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_chemicals">Production of chemicals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/300px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/450px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/600px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of chemicals from coal</div></div></div>
<p>Chemicals have been produced from coal since the 1950s. Coal can be used as a feedstock in the production of a wide range of chemical fertilizers and other chemical products. The main route to these products was <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. Primary chemicals that are produced directly from the syngas include <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which are the chemical building blocks from which a whole spectrum of derivative chemicals are manufactured, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefins" title="Olefins">olefins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and others. The versatility of <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> as a precursor to primary chemicals and high-value derivative products provides the option of using coal to produce a wide range of commodities. In the 21st century, however, the use of <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coal bed methane</a> is becoming more important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the slate of chemical products that can be made via coal gasification can in general also use feedstocks derived from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, the chemical industry tends to use whatever feedstocks are most cost-effective. Therefore, interest in using coal tended to increase for higher oil and natural gas prices and during periods of high global economic growth that might have strained oil and gas production.
</p><p>Coal to chemical processes require substantial quantities of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Much coal to chemical production is in China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OILDRUM9371_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-OILDRUM9371-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICIS022712_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICIS022712-90">[90]</a></sup> where coal dependent provinces such as <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> are struggling to control its pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity_generation">Electricity generation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_density">Energy density</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_value_of_coal" title="Energy value of coal">Energy value of coal</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of coal is roughly 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> per kilogram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> (approximately 6.7 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> per kg). For a coal power plant with a 40% efficiency, it takes an estimated 325 kg (717 lb) of coal to power a 100 W lightbulb for one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>27.6% of world energy was supplied by coal in 2017 and Asia used almost three-quarters of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precombustion_treatment">Precombustion treatment</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refined_coal" title="Refined coal">Refined coal</a></div>
<p>Refined coal is the product of a coal-upgrading technology that removes moisture and certain pollutants from lower-rank coals such as sub-bituminous and lignite (brown) coals. It is one form of several precombustion treatments and processes for coal that alter coal's characteristics before it is burned. Thermal efficiency improvements are achievable by improved pre-drying (especially relevant with high-moisture fuel such as lignite or biomass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The goals of precombustion coal technologies are to increase efficiency and reduce emissions when the coal is burned. Precombustion technology can sometimes be used as a supplement to postcombustion technologies to control emissions from coal-fueled boilers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_plant_combustion">Power plant combustion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal-fired_power_station" title="Coal-fired power station">Coal-fired power station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1439" data-file-width="2293" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/220px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/330px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/440px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant" title="Castle Gate Power Plant">Castle Gate Power Plant</a> near Helper, Utah, US</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="897" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/220px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/330px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/440px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal rail cars</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="4296" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/220px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/330px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/440px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bulldozer" title="Bulldozer">Bulldozer</a> pushing coal in <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana_Power_Station" title="Ljubljana Power Station">Ljubljana Power Station</a>, Slovenia</div></div></div>
<p>Coal burnt as a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_coal_power_stations" title="List of coal power stations">coal power stations</a> to <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generate electricity</a> is called <b>thermal coal</b>. Coal is also used to produce very high temperatures through combustion. Early deaths due to air pollution have been estimated at 200 per GW-year, however they may be higher around power plants where scrubbers are not used or lower if they are far from cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Efforts around the world to reduce the use of coal have led some regions to switch to natural gas and electricity from lower carbon sources.
</p><p>When coal is used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, it is usually pulverized and then burned in a furnace with a <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boiler</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Pulverized_coal-fired_boiler" title="Pulverized coal-fired boiler">Pulverized coal-fired boiler</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-97">[97]</a></sup> The furnace heat converts boiler water to <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>, which is then used to spin <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> which turn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">generators</a> and create electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_efficiency" title="Thermodynamic efficiency">thermodynamic efficiency</a> of this process varies between about 25% and 50% depending on the pre-combustion treatment, turbine technology (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_steam_generator" title="Supercritical steam generator">supercritical steam generator</a>) and the age of the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) power plants have been built, which burn coal more efficiently. Instead of pulverizing the coal and burning it directly as fuel in the steam-generating boiler, the <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal is gasified</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, which is burned in a <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> to produce electricity (just like natural gas is burned in a turbine). Hot exhaust gases from the turbine are used to raise steam in a <a href="/wiki/Heat_recovery_steam_generator" title="Heat recovery steam generator">heat recovery steam generator</a> which powers a supplemental <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>. The overall plant efficiency when used to provide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_heat_and_power" title="Combined heat and power">combined heat and power</a> can reach as much as 94%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> IGCC power plants emit less local pollution than conventional pulverized coal-fueled plants; however the technology for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> after gasification and before burning has so far proved to be too expensive to use with coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Other ways to use coal are as <a href="/wiki/Coal-water_slurry_fuel" title="Coal-water slurry fuel">coal-water slurry fuel</a> (CWS), which was developed in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, or in <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamic_generator" title="Magnetohydrodynamic generator">an MHD topping cycle</a>. However these are not widely used due to lack of profit.
</p><p>In 2017 38% of the world's electricity came from coal, the same percentage as 30 years previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In 2018 global installed capacity was 2<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt" title="Terawatt">TW</a> (of which 1TW is in China) which was 30% of total electricity generation capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018-105">[105]</a></sup> The most dependent major country is South Africa, with over 80% of its electricity generated by coal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> but China alone generates more than half of the world's coal-generated electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peak_coal" title="Peak coal">Maximum use</a> of coal was reached in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2019_overview_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-2019_overview-108">[108]</a></sup> In 2018 coal-fired power station <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> averaged 51%, that is they operated for about half their available operating hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_industry">Coal industry</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main pages: <a href="/wiki/Category:Coal_companies" title="Category:Coal companies">Category:Coal companies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_by_country" title="Coal by country">Coal by country</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_China" title="Coal industry in China">Coal industry in China</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_Pakistan" title="Coal industry in Pakistan">Coal industry in Pakistan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_India" title="Coal industry in India">Coal industry in India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_companies_of_Australia" title="Coal companies of Australia">Coal companies of Australia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a></div>
<p>About 8000 Mt of coal are produced annually, about 90% of which is hard coal and 10% lignite. As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Coal&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> just over half is from underground mines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> More accidents occur during underground mining than surface mining. Not all countries publish <a href="/wiki/Mining_accident" title="Mining accident">mining accident</a> statistics so worldwide figures are uncertain, but it is thought that most deaths occur in <a href="/wiki/List_of_coal_mining_accidents_in_China" title="List of coal mining accidents in China">coal mining accidents in China</a>: in 2017 there were 375 coal mining related deaths in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Most coal mined is thermal coal (also called steam coal as it is used to make steam to generate electricity) but metallurgical coal (also called "metcoal" or "coking coal" as it is used to make coke to make iron) accounts for 10% to 15% of global coal use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_traded_commodity">As a traded commodity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Art_work_of_Toledo,_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_(page_125)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="5659" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Art_work_of_Toledo%2C_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_%28page_125%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Art_work_of_Toledo,_Ohio_-_DPLA_-_0a107364e8d8eb430ebc183d28c46463_(page_125)_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extensive coal docks seen in <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo, Ohio</a>, 1895</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_China" title="Coal mining in China">China mines</a> almost half the world's coal, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> with about a tenth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> accounts for about a third of world coal exports, followed by <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Indonesia#Coal" title="Energy in Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Russia" title="Coal in Russia">Russia</a>, while the largest importers are <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Japan" title="Energy in Japan">Japan</a> and India.
</p><p>The price of metallurgical coal is volatile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and much higher than the price of thermal coal because metallurgical coal must be lower in sulfur and requires more cleaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Coal futures contracts provide coal producers and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_industry" title="Electric power industry">electric power industry</a> an important tool for <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a>.
</p><p>In some countries new onshore <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a> or <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar</a> generation already costs less than coal power from existing plants (see <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">Cost of electricity by source</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> 
However, for China this is forecast for the early 2020s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarbTrk2018_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-CarbTrk2018-118">[118]</a></sup> and for southeast Asia not until the late 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In India building new plants is uneconomic and, despite being subsidized, existing plants are losing market share to renewables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_trends">Market trends</span></h3>
<p>Of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_coal_production" title="List of countries by coal production">countries which produce coal</a> China mines by far the most, almost half the world's coal, followed by less than 10% by India. China is also by far the largest consumer. Therefore, market trends depend on <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_China" title="Energy policy of China">Chinese energy policy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA2018_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA2018-121">[121]</a></sup> Although the effort to reduce pollution means that the global long-term trend is to burn less coal, the short and medium term trends may differ, in part due to Chinese financing of new coal-fired power plants in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cornot-Gandolfe_2018-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="World_coal_reserves"></span>
</p>
